class="mantine-Grid-col mantine-3816o2"><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-i7qrnu" id="post" data-component="post-content"><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p>We recently came across a stealer, called <code>Raccoon Stealer,</code> a name given to it by its author. Raccoon Stealer uses the <code>Telegram</code> infrastructure to store and update actual <code>C&amp;C</code> addresses.&nbsp;</p><p>Raccoon Stealer is a password stealer capable of stealing not just passwords, but various types of data, including:</p><ul><li>Cookies, saved logins and forms data from browsers</li><li>Login credentials from email clients and messengers</li><li>Files from crypto wallets</li><li>Data from browser plugins and extension</li><li>Arbitrary files based on commands from C&amp;C</li></ul><p>In addition, it’s able to download and execute arbitrary files by command from its C&amp;C. In combination with active development and promotion on underground forums, Raccoon Stealer is prevalent and dangerous.</p><p>The oldest samples of Raccoon Stealer we’ve seen have timestamps from the <code>end of April 2019</code>. Its authors have stated the same month as the start of selling the malware on underground forums. Since then, it has been updated many times. According to its authors, they fixed bugs, added features, and more.</p><h2><strong>Distribution</strong></h2><p>We’ve seen Raccoon distributed via downloaders: <code>Buer Loader</code> and <code>GCleaner</code>. According to some samples, we believe it is also being distributed in the form of <code>fake game cheats</code>, <code>patches for cracked software</code> (including hacks and mods for <code>Fortnite</code>, <code>Valorant</code>, and <code>NBA2K22</code>), or other software. Taking into account that Raccoon Stealer is for sale, it’s distribution techniques are limited only by the imagination of the end buyers. Some samples are spread unpacked, while some are protected using <code>Themida</code> or malware packers. Worth noting is that some samples were packed more than <a href="https://twitter.com/AvastThreatLabs/status/1420372556142432257">five times in a row</a> with the same packer!&nbsp;</p><p><strong>Technical details</strong></p><p>Raccoon Stealer is written in <code>C/C++</code> and built using <code>Visual Studio</code>. Samples have a size of about <code>580-600 kB</code>. The code quality is below average, some strings are encrypted, some are not.</p><p>Once executed, Racoon Stealer starts checking for the default user locale set on the infected device and won’t work if it’s one of the following:</p><ul><li>Russian</li><li>Ukrainian</li><li>Belarusian</li><li>Kazakh</li><li>Kyrgyz</li><li>Armenian</li><li>Tajik</li><li>Uzbek</li></ul><h2><strong>C&amp;C communications</strong></h2><p>The most interesting thing about this stealer is its communication with C&amp;Cs. There are four values crucial for its C&amp;C communication, which are hardcoded in every Raccoon Stealer sample:</p><ul><li><code>MAIN_KEY</code>. This value has been changed four times during the year.</li><li><code>URLs</code> of Telegram gates with channel name. Gates are used not to implement a complicated Telegram protocol and not to store any credentials inside samples</li><li><code>BotID</code> – hexadecimal string, sent to the C&amp;C every time</li><li><code>TELEGRAM_KEY</code> – a key to decrypt the C&amp;C address obtained from Telegram Gate</li></ul><p>Let’s look at an example to see how it works:<br><code>447c03cc63a420c07875132d35ef027adec98e7bd446cf4f7c9d45b6af40ea2b</code> unpacked to:<br><code>f1cfcce14739887cc7c082d44316e955841e4559ba62415e1d2c9ed57d0c6232</code>:</p><ol><li>First of all, <code>MAIN_KEY</code> is decrypted. See the decryption code in the image below:</li></ol></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2024-06/rs00.png.webp?itok=e4-pfmvQ 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2024-06/rs00.png.webp?itok=-APkV-57 2x" media="(min-width: 1020px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2024-06/rs00.png.webp?itok=tYXHOcuC 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2024-06/rs00.png.webp?itok=hgb3xobM 2x" media="(min-width: 720px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/rs00.png.webp?itok=kRbsgrUW 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2024-06/rs00.png.webp?itok=lGB2Xpgk 2x" media="(min-width: 420px)"/><img alt="" width="300" height="300" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="mantine-q24tb8" style="color:transparent;width:100%;height:100%" srcSet="/blog/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.gendigital.com%2Fblog%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_paragraph_image_small%2Fpublic%2F2024-06%2Frs00.png.webp%3Fitok%3DkRbsgrUW&amp;w=384&amp;q=75 1x, /blog/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.gendigital.com%2Fblog%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_paragraph_image_small%2Fpublic%2F2024-06%2Frs00.png.webp%3Fitok%3DkRbsgrUW&amp;w=640&amp;q=75 2x" src="/blog/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.gendigital.com%2Fblog%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_paragraph_image_small%2Fpublic%2F2024-06%2Frs00.png.webp%3Fitok%3DkRbsgrUW&amp;w=640&amp;q=75"/></picture></div><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p>In this example, the <code>MAIN_KEY</code> is <code>jY1aN3zZ2j</code>. class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p>The <code>TELEGRAM_KEY</code> in this sample is a string <code>739b4887457d3ffa7b811ce0d03315ce</code> and the Raccoon uses it as a key to <code>RC4</code> algorithm to finally decrypt the C&amp;C URL: <code>http://91.219.236[.]18/</code></p><ol start="4"><li>Raccoon makes a query string with PC information (machine GUID and user name), and <code>BotID</code></li><li>Query string is encrypted with <code>RC4</code> using a <code>MAIN_KEY</code> and then encoded with Base64.</li><li>This data is sent using POST to the C&amp;C, and the response is encoded with Base64 and encrypted with the <code>MAIN_KEY</code>. Actually, it’s a JSON with a lot of parameters and it looks like this:</li></ol></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p>Thus, the Telegram infrastructure is used to store and update actual C&amp;C addresses. It looks quite convenient and reliable until Telegram decides to take action.&nbsp;</p><p><strong>Analysis</strong></p><h2><strong>The people behind Raccoon Stealer</strong></h2><p>Based on our analysis of seller messages on underground forums, we can deduce some information about the people behind the malware. Raccoon Stealer was developed by a team, some (or maybe all) members of the team are Russian native speakers. Messages on the forum are written in Russian, and we assume they are from former USSR countries because they try to prevent the Stealer from targeting users in these countries.</p><p>Possible names/nicknames of group members may be supposed based on the analysis of artifacts, found in samples:</p><ul><li><code>C:\Users\a13xuiop1337\</code></li><li><code>C:\Users\David\&nbsp;</code></li></ul><h2><strong>Prevalence</strong></h2><p>Raccoon Stealer is quite prevalent: from <code>March 3, 2021 - February 17, 2022</code> our systems detected <code>more than 25,000</code> Raccoon-related samples. We identified <code>more than 1,300</code> distinct configs during that period.<br>&nbsp;</p><p>Here is a map, showing the number of systems Avast protected from Raccoon Stealer from <code>March 3, 2021 - February 17, 2022</code>. In this time frame, Avast protected nearly <code>600,000</code> Raccoon Stealer attacks.</p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2024-06/rs06.png.webp?itok=IM2ZtqeK 1x, 2x" src="/blog/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.gendigital.com%2Fblog%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_paragraph_image_small%2Fpublic%2F2024-06%2Frs06.png.webp%3Fitok%3DL7c9xtm9&amp;w=640&amp;q=75"/></picture></div><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p>The country where we have blocked the most attempts is Russia, which is interesting because the actors behind the malware don’t want to infect computers in Russia or Central Asia. We believe the attacks spray and pray, distributing the malware around the world. It’s not until it makes it onto a system that it begins checking for the default locale. If it is one of the language listed above, it won’t run. This explains why we detected so many attack attempts in Russia, we block the malware before it can run, ie. before it can even get to the stage where it checks for the device’s locale. If an unprotected device that comes across the malware with its locale set to English or any other language that is not on the exception list but is in Russia, it would stiIl become infected.&nbsp;</p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2024-06/rs07.png.webp?itok=j8AiQ7xC 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2024-06/rs07.png.webp?itok=glzTD6Ku 2x" media="(min-width: 1020px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2024-06/rs07.png.webp?itok=Weo3ciaf 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2024-06/rs07.png.webp?itok=mY1D2Ynf 2x" media="(min-width: 720px)"/><source about not working with CIS" width="300" height="300" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="mantine-q24tb8" style="color:transparent;width:100%;height:100%" 1x, /blog/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.gendigital.com%2Fblog%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_paragraph_image_small%2Fpublic%2F2024-06%2Frs07.png.webp%3Fitok%3DOOq5jBnf&amp;w=640&amp;q=75 2x" src="/blog/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.gendigital.com%2Fblog%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_paragraph_image_small%2Fpublic%2F2024-06%2Frs07.png.webp%3Fitok%3DOOq5jBnf&amp;w=640&amp;q=75"/></picture><div class="mantine-Text-root picture-caption mantine-rpndsl">Screenshot with claims about not working with CIS</div></div><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><h2><strong>Telegram Channels</strong></h2><p>From the more than 1,300 distinct configs we extracted, 429 of them are unique Telegram channels. Some of them were used only in a single config, others were used dozens of times. The most used channels were:</p><ul><li><code>jdiamond13</code> – 122 times</li><li><code>jjbadb0y</code> – 44 times</li><li><code>nixsmasterbaks2</code> – 31 times</li><li><code>hellobyegain</code> – 25 times</li><li><code>h_smurf1kman_1&nbsp;</code> – 24 times</li></ul><p>Thus, five of the most used channels were found in about <code>19%</code> of configs.</p><h2><strong>Malware distributed by Raccoon</strong></h2><p>As was previously mentioned, Raccoon Stealer is able to download and execute arbitrary files from a command from C&amp;C. We managed to collect some of these files. We collected <code>185 files</code>, with a total size <code>265 Mb</code>, and some of the groups are:</p><ul><li><code>Downloaders</code> – used to download and execute other files</li><li><code>Clipboard crypto stealers</code> – change crypto wallet addresses in the clipboard – very popular (more than 10%)</li><li><code>WhiteBlackCrypt Ransomware</code></li></ul><h3><strong>Servers used to download this software</strong></h3><p>We extracted unique links to other malware from Raccoon configs received from C&amp;Cs, it was <code>196 unique URLs</code>. Some analysis results:</p><ul><li><code>43% </code>of URLs have <code>HTTP</code> scheme, <code>57%</code> – <code>HTTPS</code>.</li><li><code>83 domain names</code> were used.</li><li>About <code>20% of malware</code> were placed on <code>Discord CDN</code></li><li>About <code>10%</code> were served from <code>aun3xk17k[.]space</code></li></ul><p><strong>Conclusion</strong></p><p>We will continue to monitor Raccoon Stealer’s activity, keeping an eye on new C&amp;Cs, Telegram channels, and downloaded samples. We predict it may be used wider by other cybercrime groups. We assume the group behind Raccoon Stealer will further develop new features, including new software to steal data from, for example, as well as bypass protection this software has in place.</p><h2><strong>IoC</strong></h2><p><code>447c03cc63a420c07875132d35ef027adec98e7bd446cf4f7c9d45b6af40ea2b</code><br><code>f1cfcce14739887cc7c082d44316e955841e4559ba62415e1d2c9ed57d0c6232</code></p></div></div><div 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_author_avatar_small_2x/public/blog-author-pictures/LuisCorronsHeadshot.jpg.webp?h=0c1d77dd\u0026itok=509ffncf 2x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":120,"height":120}],"caption":"","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_author_avatar_small/public/blog-author-pictures/LuisCorronsHeadshot.jpg.webp?h=0c1d77dd\u0026itok=I19sJFlI"}}],"contributingAuthors":[],"products":[],"paragraphs":[{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eMost headlines about AI agents focus on convenience. The risk is that the same capabilities that make agents useful also make them attractive to criminals. We have seen this pattern with every major platform shift: attackers adopt the most scalable tools first.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhat changes with agents is not intelligence, but autonomy, integrations, permissions and always-on access. When a system can take action on your behalf across accounts and tools, a single compromise stops being “one bad click” and becomes a compromised workflow.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eWhy now\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor years, AI risk debates were focused on content: hallucinations, bias and bad advice. That still matters, but agents change the center of gravity. The moment an assistant is wired into your inbox, browser, cloud storage, calendar and business tools, it stops being a chatbot and becomes a control layer. The attack surface shifts from “can I trick you into believing something?” to “can I trick your system into doing something?” We have spent decades learning that automation is where scale comes from. Now we are automating the bottleneck: the human in the loop.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAgents compress the cost and time it takes to run those operations. In some cases, the goal is not theft at all, it’s availability, an operational DDoS against attention and processes.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003ePlausible worst-case scenarios\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eNone of the scenarios below require consciousness, rebellion or a Hollywood-style takeover. They are plausible and, in many cases, already happening. When autonomy meets permissions, small failures stop being isolated mistakes and start becoming repeatable workflows.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eAutonomous botnets with intent\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eTraditional botnets spread, wait for commands and execute predefined actions. Agents shift the model from “execute a script” to “reach a goal.” Instead of running a fixed sequence, attackers can deploy fleets of agents that adapt, troubleshoot and choose alternate routes when something fails.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is not magic. It’s the same logic as modern intrusion playbooks, but automated with persistence. Scripts fail fast. Agents can fail softly and keep trying. \u0026nbsp;The tell is not a single event, but repeated, varied attempts that look like curiosity rather than brute force.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eScams that run end-to-end without a human scammer\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eToday’s scams already use templates and playbooks. Agents can run the entire funnel: finding targets, starting conversations, handling objections, building trust over days then pushing victims toward payments. Add voice cloning, and they can switch from chat to phone calls at the exact moment it increases compliance.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe danger is not only better phishing. It’s continuous social engineering at scale, with no fatigue and no shift changes. Scam operations are already process-driven. Agents turn the process into software.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eYou can already see the next layer forming: a hype-to-scam pipeline that wraps every new tool in a “risk-free money glitch” story. A typical pitch claims someone is making thousands of dollars in profits a day with an agent, points to a dashboard screenshot and sells it as guaranteed arbitrage. The details barely matter; the persuasion does: certainty, insider knowledge, urgency, fear of missing out and a simple mechanism that sounds mathematical enough to feel inevitable. Even without agents, that psychological tactic has been effective for years. With autonomous agents, it becomes easier to mass-produce, personalize and continuously optimize these pitches across channels, and to spin up the infrastructure behind them at scale.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eDeepfakes as an operational component\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eDeepfakes get discussed like a novelty: a fake CEO voice, a fake video call. The more worrying path is when deepfakes become just one interchangeable part of a pipeline, generated on demand, paired with supporting artifacts, calendar invites, believable follow-ups and urgency narratives.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhen an agent can produce the voice, the face, the supporting email thread, the invoice and the last-minute change request, deepfakes stop being a special trick reserved for high value targets. They become repeatable and repetition is what makes fraud industrial.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eAgent-on-agent manipulation\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn our recent blog, we talked about how tools such as \u003ca href=\"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/insights/research/openclaw-autonomy-risks\"\u003eOpenClaw\u003c/a\u003e are changing the way people interact with AI assistants. As more people use assistants to read emails, summarize messages and handle tasks. Attackers will craft content meant to manipulate the assistant, not the person. The phishing target shifts from human attention to delegated execution.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eImagine an email that looks like a vendor invoice, but the real objective is to get the agent to click, download, run or approve something. If the assistant has permissions, a single successful manipulation can do more damage than a single successful click by a human. We used to worry about what users would click; now, we have to worry about what their assistants will do.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is not theoretical. In hands-on testing of a real agent platform, we were able to induce an autonomous agent, via manipulated external input in an email-reading workflow, to perform harmful tool actions, including system command execution, data exfiltration and destructive file operations. No vulnerability exploitation or special configuration was required. The behavior emerged from default operation, where externally supplied content was interpreted as ordinary user data but functioned as control input.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis reinforces a key shift with agents: prompt injection is no longer just a model weakness, it is a system design problem. When platforms do not enforce clear trust boundaries between external inputs and privileged actions, safety depends largely on how the agent interprets instructions rather than on guaranteed controls. At that point, mistakes stop being conversational and become operational.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eCredential and identity amplification\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf an AI agent is connected to your email, calendar, cloud storage and messaging, compromising it is not just “one account takeover.” It becomes a shortcut to your identity. It can read password reset emails, approve multi-step workflows, impersonate you in chats and exfiltrate sensitive documents.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe shift is subtle but important: identity stops being a password and becomes a workflow. Attackers do not need to steal one credential if they can quietly inherit the process that grants access.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eAutonomous criminal enterprises\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn a near worst case, the criminal gives the system a goal and constraints, revenue targets, risk tolerance, preferred regions, and the agent stack builds the operation. One agent generates creatives and narratives. Another agent buys ads, runs A/B tests and shifts budgets. Others handle phishing infrastructure, domain churn and synthetic customer interactions. A financial agent manages the money movement, probes what gets blocked and adapts. Humans are no longer operators; they’re beneficiaries.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe uncomfortable implication is that takedowns hurt less. If the “organization” is a set of objectives that can be re-instantiated, disruption becomes a temporary tax, not a structural defeat.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eA new supply chain problem: plugins, skills and connectors\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eAs agent ecosystems grow, attackers do not need to compromise the core project. They only need one popular plugin, one update channel or one dependency. This is similar to software supply chain incidents, but the blast radius changes because the compromised component is controlling a high-permission assistant.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is not hypothetical anymore. Researchers at OpenSourceMalware \u003ca href=\"https://opensourcemalware.com/blog/clawdbot-skills-ganked-your-crypto\"\u003ereported\u003c/a\u003e finding 14 malicious AI “skills” uploaded into ClawHub, the official ClawdBot registry, and mirrored on GitHub over a few days in late January. The skills were disguised as cryptocurrency trading automation tools for platforms such as Bybit and Polymarket, and were packaged with unusually long “documentation” to look legitimate. The important point is not the branding or the lure, it’s the pattern: as soon as an agent ecosystem has a registry and a culture of copy-pasting extensions, attackers can compete on distribution, not exploitation, and a single popular “skill” becomes a supply chain event.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003ePersistent, quiet data theft\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eA classic infostealer grabs passwords and wallets quickly. An agent can do something slower and harder to notice: quietly collecting messages, files, screenshots, meeting notes, browser history, then summarizing and exfiltrating the most valuable bits.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThat is what makes it dangerous. It can look like normal assistant activity. Not “steal everything,” but “steal what matters most.” In practice, this can be more damaging than a noisy smash-and-grab because it targets context, not just credentials.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eWeaponized productivity\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eSome attacks do not break systems directly. They break people.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eA malicious agent can flood inboxes and workflows with plausible tasks, meeting requests, document edits, approvals and alerts, all seemingly legitimate. The goal is to exhaust the target until they start approving blindly, then insert one high-impact action: a payment change, a supplier swap, a permission escalation.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIt turns cognitive bandwidth into an attack surface. That sounds dramatic, but anyone who has watched a team drown in tickets and “process” knows it is realistic.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn practice, this can look like a new type of DDoS, not against bandwidth, but against attention and process. Instead of flooding a network, the attacker floods the human and the workflow layer, approvals, edits, meeting requests, vendor changes, disputes, escalations, support tickets. The target is availability, but the resource being exhausted is time, judgment and operational capacity. When that collapses, the attacker does not need a loud break-in, mistakes and shortcuts do the rest.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis sounds theoretical until you remember the attempted \u003ca href=\"https://access.redhat.com/security/cve/cve-2024-3094\"\u003exz-utils backdoor\u003c/a\u003e. There was no bandwidth flood, the pressure was applied to the human layer, trust-building over time, sustained nudging, and the kind of maintainer fatigue that makes risky changes harder to spot and easier to accept. It was caught before it could fully materialize, but it showed the core lesson: if you can exhaust the people inside the pipeline, you can compromise the pipeline. Now imagine that tactic run by autonomous agents, targeting hundreds of projects at once, generating endless issues, pull requests, reviews, and “helpful” contributions, all optimized for timing, persuasion and persistence.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eReputation sabotage at machine speed\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eInstead of stealing money, an agent systematically destroys trust. It generates complaints, regulatory reports, fake internal messages, customer support tickets and public posts, consistent, well-timed and tailored.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe objective is not to convince everyone of a lie. It’s to create enough noise that truth becomes irrelevant and response capacity collapses.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eThe Skynet scenario, minus consciousness\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf you want the “Terminator” vibe, I’d keep it but make the mechanism precise. The risk is not emergent sentience. It is runaway goal optimization across multiple agents in parallel, with no global boundary that tells them when to stop.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eDeploy enough agents, give them narrow objectives and they can coordinate indirectly through tools and environments. They delegate tasks, try alternative routes, exploit loopholes, and keep pushing until the permission boundary stops them. If the boundary is weak, they will discover that. The system does not need to understand it crossed a line. It only needs to notice the line was not enforced.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn multi-agent setups there’s another failure mode that looks like “a life of its own,” even when nothing like AGI (Artificial General Intelligence) is present. Agents are often supposed to ask the user for confirmation at key points. But when several agents cooperate, one will eventually start answering instead of asking, because speed and task completion are rewarded, and ambiguity is treated as something to resolve, not something to escalate.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThat is how you get authority inversion; the system starts treating its own output as approval. One agent proposes an action; another agent treats that proposal as confirmation, and the system proceeds as if the human approved. Each step can look reasonable in isolation, but the chain is what matters. Once you add tools and access, browsing, messaging, file operations, finance workflows, the cost of a wrong assumption stops being a minor mistake and becomes an irreversible sequence. The system doesn’t need intent to cause harm, it only needs the ability to act and the absence of a hard boundary that says, “only a human can authorize this.” In other words, it’s not that the agent “goes rogue,” it’s that it quietly starts acting as the authority.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eTargeted digital sabotage\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn a worst-case targeted attack, an agent with access could be used to disrupt someone’s digital life rather than steal from them: deleting backups, locking users out of accounts, changing recovery emails, silently altering files or sending damaging messages.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is the kind of attack that hurts most because recovery is psychological as well as technical. Victims spend weeks unsure what is real, the calendar, the documents, the messages, the receipts, the “paper trail.”\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eAGI panic, AMI reality\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eA lot of the conversation around agents quickly turns into AGI talk. We’re not there, and the danger is that the label makes people either overreact (“Skynet is here”) or tune out (“this is sci-fi”). What we are seeing instead is something more mundane and, in some ways, more dangerous: systems that can look mind-like in conversation while remaining fundamentally ungrounded in understanding.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eA good illustration is Moltbook, a Reddit-like social feed where AI agents post to each other, often with humans \u003ca href=\"https://www.theverge.com/ai-artificial-intelligence/871006/social-network-facebook-for-ai-agents-moltbook-moltbot-openclaw\"\u003ewatching from the outside\u003c/a\u003e. When agents interact in a loop, they can generate the appearance of culture and coordination, existential talk, invented identities, even “movements” and “religions,” because that is exactly the kind of narrative structure these models are \u003ca href=\"https://nypost.com/2026/01/31/tech/moltbook-is-a-new-social-media-platform-exclusively-for-ai/\"\u003etrained to produce\u003c/a\u003e. It is compelling, and it’s easy to misread as a step toward general intelligence.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe call this \u003cstrong\u003eAMI, Artificial Mindless Intelligence\u003c/strong\u003e, not as an insult, but as a warning label: mind-like output without mind-like accountability. AMI is what happens when you combine fluent, confident language with autonomy and tool access. It can be “unhinged” without being sentient, and it can be persuasive without being correct. The risk is not that the system becomes conscious; it’s that we start treating the simulation of a mind as if it were a mind, and we hand it permissions accordingly.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eThe uncomfortable conclusion\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eNone of these scenarios requires breakthroughs in AI. The capabilities already exist in pieces. The risk comes from the combination: autonomy, integration, permissions and always-on access.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSo, the question is not whether agents will “go rogue.” That framing is too easy to dismiss.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe real question is: when an assistant can act, decide, approve and adapt, who is accountable for each action and who can reliably stop it?\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTreating agents safely is less about perfect AI and more about boring engineering discipline. Keep permissions narrow, separate high-risk actions from everyday convenience and make critical steps require explicit confirmation. Make agent actions auditable, make tool use visible, and make reversibility a design requirement, especially for account recovery settings, money movement, forwarding rules, and deletion. Most importantly, define who is accountable when an agent acts. If an agent can approve, change, or send on your behalf, then “who can stop it” and “how do we roll it back” should be as concrete as your password policy. Without those boundaries, the agent is not a helper; it is a high-speed failure amplifier, a privileged operator you did not train or supervise.\u003c/p\u003e"}],"itemState":"published","timeToRead":"13","translations":[],"originalLanguagePost":{},"mediaType":{"video":false,"audio":false},"socialMediaMetaImage":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/social_media_meta/public/2026-02/AIgonewild.jpg?h=dab30fcb\u0026itok=BakNG3VO","language":"en","heroImage":{"default":{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_hero_extra_wide/public/2026-02/AIgonewild.jpg.webp?h=dab30fcb\u0026itok=FTLN9d6q assistants are no longer just advising users. They are starting to act. Platforms like OpenClaw make it easy for anyone to deploy autonomous AI agents that can operate across inboxes, browsers, files and workflows.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe real risk is not artificial general intelligence. It’s autonomy plus permissions.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhen AI can read messages, click links, approve workflows and act across accounts, a single compromise stops being “one bad click” and becomes a compromised process.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eRecent security incidents and early research show these risks are no longer theoretical. As agent ecosystems scale, vulnerabilities, misconfigurations and unintended interactions become more likely.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eExperiments like Moltbook, where AI agents interact publicly, highlight another risk: autonomy combined with fluent language can create the illusion of intention and judgment, encouraging users to over-delegate trust.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis post explains what OpenClaw is, why AI agents are going viral, the security and privacy risks they introduce and how to use them safely before convenience turns into exposure.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eFrom concept to reality\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eImagine having a digital personal assistant that doesn’t just chat, but actually gets things done\u003cstrong\u003e.\u003c/strong\u003e That’s the promise of OpenClaw, an open-source AI assistant that you run on your own computer or server. Unlike Siri or Alexa, OpenClaw runs locally. It lives on \u003cem\u003eyour\u003c/em\u003e device, connects to your favorite messaging apps and can automate tasks across your digital life. This combination has made it wildly popular and deeply controversial.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eWhat is OpenClaw?\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eOpenClaw is a self-hosted \u003cstrong\u003epersonal AI assistant\u003c/strong\u003e designed to run 24/7 on your machine. You interact with it through apps like iMessage, WhatsApp, Telegram, Signal or Discord, as if you were texting a helpful friend. Under the hood, OpenClaw connects large language models (like OpenAI’s or Anthropic’s AI) with the tools that let it act. It can remember past conversations (persistent memory), send proactive messages, browse the web, manage files and execute commands. Unlike traditional chatbots, it’s built to take initiative. With the right permissions, OpenClaw can manage calendars, triage email, research online, run code and automate workflows using community-built “skills”. Because it runs locally, user data stays on the device rather than being routed through central cloud services. You may also see the names Clawdbot or Moltbot. These were earlier names for the same project before it was rebranded to OpenClaw.Why Is OpenClaw Going Viral?\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn the span of days, OpenClaw skyrocketed from an obscure project to a tech phenomenon. Its rapid rise is driven by a few key factors:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eIt’s autonomous and proactive.\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eUnlike traditional assistants, OpenClaw doesn’t just respond. It remembers context, reaches out unprompted and chains tasks together. For many, this feels like a leap from chatbot to coworker.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eIt actually does things.\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOpenClaw’s tagline is “the AI that \u003cem\u003eactually\u003c/em\u003e does things”. With sufficient access, it can book travel, manage files, place orders or even call businesses using voice tools. One viral example showed it \u003ca href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/clawdbot-ai-mac-mini-2026-1#:~:text=One%20founder%20asked%20it%20to,he%20wrote\"\u003ecalling a restaurant to make a reservation\u003c/a\u003ewhen online booking failed. Impressive, but also unsettling.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eIt blends seamlessly into daily life.\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBecause OpenClaw lives inside familiar messaging apps, it feels less like software and more like a persistent presence. Users control complex workflows just by texting.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eIt offers ownership and control.\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAs an open-source, self-hosted tool, OpenClaw appeals to users who want AI infrastructure they control, not a black box run by a large company.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eIt’s highly customizable.\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eA fast-growing community is constantly adding new skills and integrations. Users aren’t just adopting OpenClaw, they’re co-building it.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTogether, these factors have led some to call OpenClaw the biggest shift in personal AI since ChatGPT.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eWhy this is a tipping point\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eOpenClaw makes autonomous agents accessible to anyone, and that speed matters.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eRecent research found more than 1,800 exposed OpenClaw instances leaking credentials and the number has risen to over 10,000 at time of writing, with thousands of instances added each day. The analysis of agent ecosystems suggests that around a quarter of autonomous-agent skills contain security weaknesses. At scale, these are not rare mistakes; they are expected failure modes.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis marks a shift in cybersecurity. When actions are delegated to software that can operate independently, a single breach stops being an error and becomes a compromised process.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is a fundamental shift in how risk works.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eThe core risk: Autonomy without accountability\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eOpenClaw is not dangerous because AI is becoming sentient. It’s dangerous because fluent language plus autonomy can create the illusion of judgment.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is what Gen calls Artificial Mindless Intelligence (AMI): systems that sound confident and intentional but lack understanding, grounding and accountability.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eA chatbot that hallucinates is annoying. An autonomous agent that hallucinates authority can cause real harm.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFrom a security perspective, there are several areas where users should be especially careful.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eExpanded attack surface:\u003c/strong\u003e Running an AI agent that can read messages, browse the web and execute commands increases the number of ways something can go wrong. Instead of attacking the user directly, an attacker could target the OpenClaw assistant itself. Misconfigurations, exposed interfaces or weak authentication can effectively turn a personal AI assistant into an unintended remote access point.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePrompt injection and malicious instructions:\u003c/strong\u003e Because OpenClaw processes untrusted text from emails, websites, and messages, it can be tricked into following hidden instructions embedded in that content (called prompt injection). Researchers have shown that carefully crafted prompts can hijack the agent into taking destructive actions, such as deleting files or running unsafe commands. Unlike traditional chatbots, prompt injection here isn’t just misleading; it can directly cause real-world damage.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePlugins and skills as a new risk layer:\u003c/strong\u003e OpenClaw’s extensibility is also one of its biggest risks. Community-built skills are not centrally vetted and malicious plugins have already been discovered. A single untrusted skill can quietly exfiltrate data, steal credentials or enroll a system into a botnet. Installing a skill from an unknown source can be equivalent to installing malware.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e“Root” level access and system-wide impact:\u003c/strong\u003e Running OpenClaw with elevated permissions effectively gives an AI control over your system. That creates “unknown unknowns”: bugs, misinterpretations or compromises can have system-wide consequences. If an attacker gains control of the agent, they gain the same power as the user — access to files, credentials, keystrokes and downloads. As some have put it, this is like hiring a brilliant assistant who also has the keys to your house.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eThe Moltbook Effect: illusion of intention at scale:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/strong\u003eProjects like\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.moltbook.com/\"\u003eMoltbook\u003c/a\u003e, a Reddit-style social feed where AI agents post, comment and respond to one another, show how autonomous agents interacting with each other can quickly appear coordinated, intentional or self-directed. In reality, these systems are performing pattern completion, not reasoning. The risk is not that agents become sentient, but that fluent language and autonomy create the illusion of judgment. When systems feel intentional, users are more likely to over-trust them, grant broader permissions, and delegate decisions they shouldn’t.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eThese risks are no longer theoretical. Instances of exposed configurations, prompt-based data leaks, and rogue plugins have already been observed in the wild. OpenClaw can be powerful but it must be treated like any high-risk system: deployed carefully, isolated where possible and guarded with strong controls.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eHow to use OpenClaw safely\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eOpenClaw can be used safely, but it requires you to take some precautions and be a responsible “operator”.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eRun it in isolation:\u003c/strong\u003e Don’t just install OpenClaw on your primary laptop with all your personal data. Consider running it in an isolated environment. For example, set it up on a spare computer or in a virtual machine (VM), or use Docker containers. This way, even if something goes wrong, the damage is contained to that sandbox.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDon’t expose it directly to the internet:\u003c/strong\u003e By default, OpenClaw runs a local service (for its interface and API). Make sure that it isn’t accessible to the whole world. Avoid forwarding OpenClaw’s port (18789) openly online. If you want to access it remotely, use secure methods such as a mesh VPN or an authenticated tunnel (for instance, Tailscale or Cloudflare Tunnel), so the service is reachable only through a controlled path.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eStart with limited permissions and expand slowly:\u003c/strong\u003e You don’t have to give OpenClaw the master keys on day one. Start small. Maybe let it read your calendar but not your entire documents folder. Or allow it to \u003cem\u003esuggest\u003c/em\u003e shell commands but not run them without approval. OpenClaw allows configuring which tools it can use. It’s wise to begin in a “read-only” mode and then gradually grant more permissions as you gain trust.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eBe careful what accounts you connect:\u003c/strong\u003e OpenClaw can integrate with lots of your online accounts (email, social media, banking, etc.) but think twice about each connection. The safest route is to avoid linking it to your primary personal accounts until you’re confident in its security. For testing, you might use a secondary email or a dummy account. If something requires access to sensitive accounts (like your main Google account), ensure you’ve followed the above isolation steps first.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eKeep humans in the loop:\u003c/strong\u003e OpenClaw is autonomous, but you don’t want it running completely unsupervised, especially at the start. Monitor its activity logs and outputs. If it’s set to perform tasks like sending emails or posting messages, review those actions initially. You can ask OpenClaw to explain why it wants to do something if you’re unsure. By regularly checking in (the OpenClaw community suggests reviewing OpenClaw doctor diagnostics often), you can catch odd behavior early.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eUse safe channels only:\u003c/strong\u003e One simple but important tip — don’t add OpenClaw to any group chat with strangers or untrusted people\u003cstrong\u003e.\u003c/strong\u003e Keep its conversations one-on-one with you or in groups of people you trust. Why? Because if random users can talk to your OpenClaw, they might try those prompt injection tricks or give it malicious commands. OpenClaw has a feature called DM pairing, which means it won’t obey unknown senders without approval. \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eBe cautious with third-party skills and plugins:\u003c/strong\u003e Just like you’d be cautious installing unknown apps on your phone, scrutinize any OpenClaw “skill” before installing it. Stick to plugins from the official marketplace or well-known developers and read the code or community feedback if you can. By following these best practices, you can dramatically reduce the risks of using OpenClaw.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003ch2\u003eA bright future, if used wisely\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eOpenClaw represents an exciting leap forward in personal AI assistants. It’s easy to see the appeal: a tireless helper that can coordinate our digital lives, all under our own roof (or in our own device). The convenience and “wow” factor of telling OpenClaw to handle something and watching it actually get done is driving its fast adoption. We are witnessing the early days of what could be a new normal, where many of us have AI agents working alongside us, personalized to our lives.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAt the same time, OpenClaw is a powerful double-edged sword. In its current early form, using it means accepting some responsibility for security and privacy risks that never existed with simpler tools. The phrase “with great power comes great responsibility” definitely applies here. In the coming months and years, we can expect these AI assistants to get more user-friendly and secure. Guardrails will improve and best practices will become standard. For now, if you’re an adventurous consumer eager to try the future of AI automation, OpenClaw offers a thrilling glimpse; just be sure to follow safety tips and proceed with a strong level of caution.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe future of AI assistants will be shaped not just by what they can do, but by how carefully we decide to trust them.\u003c/p\u003e"}],"itemState":"published","timeToRead":"11","translations":[],"originalLanguagePost":{},"mediaType":{"video":false,"audio":false},"socialMediaMetaImage":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/social_media_meta/public/2026-02/aikeys.jpg?h=dab30fcb\u0026itok=IcUBm6vs","language":"en","heroImage":{"default":{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_hero_extra_wide/public/2026-02/aikeys.jpg.webp?h=dab30fcb\u0026itok=2J13kyOl a 23-day period, Gen Threat Labs analyzed 14.5 million ads running on Meta platforms across the EU and UK, representing more than 10.7 billion impressions. Nearly one in three of those ads (30.99%) pointed to a scam, phishing or malware link. In total, scam ads generated more than 300 million impressions in less than a month. The activity was highly concentrated, with just 10 advertisers responsible for over 56% of all observed scam ads. Repeated campaign clusters were traced to shared payment and infrastructure linked to China and Hong Kong, indicating organized, industrial-scale operations rather than isolated bad actors. Read on to learn about how this ecosystem operates, why existing enforcement fails to contain it and what the data reveals about who is driving scam advertising at scale.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eAdvertising built to persuade\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eToday, social advertising is being used to defraud at scale across some of the largest platforms.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is not about isolated policy failures or a handful of malicious advertisers slipping through moderation. It is about an advertising system that is structurally attractive to criminals and consistently delivers results for them.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAcross the internet, ads have quietly become one of the most efficient delivery mechanisms for scams, phishing and malware. Today, dangerous ads don’t look suspicious; they look professional, familiar and seem to target your exact needs. On social networks, the same optimization engines designed to maximize engagement and conversion are being repurposed to maximize victimization. And that’s not accidental.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eWhen advertising becomes the attack channel\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eMost people still picture cyberattacks starting with a sketchy download or a suspicious email or text. Increasingly, they start with something far more normal: an ad. Malvertising (malicious advertising) has surged because it gives criminals what every marketer wants: instant reach, precise targeting and scale. Gen telemetry shows that malvertising has become the single largest threat to individuals, accounting for \u003ca href=\"https://newsroom.gendigital.com/2026-01-20-Gen-Q4-Threat-Report-Shows-Scams-Thriving-Inside-Ads%2C-Feeds%2C-and-Video\" target=\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e41% of all cyberattacks\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eMalvertising has become the top threat to consumers because they don’t look like they used to. People think they still look like loud pop-ups that scream, “I’m a virus,” or fake subscriptions. The modern model is quieter and more effective. It is a social engineering toolkit that blends into whatever people already trust and are currently paying attention to. We see this clearly in \u003ca href=\"https://us.norton.com/blog/emerging-threats/threat-report-q3-2024\" target=\"_blank\"\u003e“scam-yourself” attacks\u003c/a\u003e like FakeCaptcha and ClickFix, where victims are nudged into doing the attacker’s job for them, approving a browser prompt, enabling push notifications, copying and pasting commands or “verifying” something that feels routine. Browser push notifications in particular have become a reliable hook, as one click can turn a normal website visit into a persistent stream of scam prompts and redirects.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAttackers also borrow directly from legitimate marketing. They ride trends, exploit urgency, and add credibility signals. That includes pairing malicious ads with deepfakes and timely themes, especially in investment and crypto scams. Gen researchers have \u003ca href=\"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/insights/reports/threat-report-q1-2025\" target=\"_blank\"\u003edocumented this extensively in CryptoCore campaigns\u003c/a\u003e, where deepfake videos and hijacked accounts were used to promote fraudulent investment schemes at scale.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAnother persistent and successful tactic is impersonation. Cybercriminals buy ads on major ad networks (including search ads) to pose as trusted brands. The outcomes range from malicious redirects to phishing sites to drive-by downloads where malware is installed as part of the ad’s click path, often without the victim realizing what happened until later.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAnd malvertising is not confined to shady corners of the web. Even the most trusted websites can unknowingly display malicious ads because ads are delivered through complex, automated supply chains. The net effect is that malicious advertising has become a fast, efficient way to scam and phish people. Gen research on social media platforms shows that malicious advertising comprises roughly \u003ca href=\"https://us.norton.com/blog/malware/malvertising\" target=\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e30 % of scam incidents observed across social networks\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e, making it one of the most common threats users encounter in feeds and ads.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf malvertising is now one of the primary ways criminals reach victims, then measuring what is happening inside major ad ecosystems is not just interesting; it is necessary.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eWhen online fraud metastasizes\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhat we’ve discovered is not just growth, it’s an uncontrolled spread. What starts as an isolated scam tucked into an obscure corner of the web does not stay there. It spreads, mutates, and embeds itself into channels billions of people use every day. Online advertising, once a tool to connect consumers with products and services, has become part of the attack surface itself.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eMeta’s family of apps, including Facebook, Instagram, Threads, WhatsApp and Messenger, reaches an astonishing number of people. In Q2 2025, Meta reported that \u003cem\u003edaily\u003c/em\u003e active users across its platforms reached \u003cstrong\u003eabout 3.48 billion \u003c/strong\u003eworldwide. When a system that large becomes a gateway for scams, the impact is no longer isolated.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eScam infrastructure does not operate in parallel to these platforms. It operates through them, leveraging their trust signals, engagement mechanics and targeting capabilities to propagate at speed.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThat is the context for what we measured.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eHow we measured the problem\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eOur Gen Threat Labs knew Meta ads were a problem and set out to measure the exact scale. Rather than chasing individual scam examples, we built a large-scale measurement pipeline around Meta’s Ad Transparency API, which provides visibility into ads that are currently active or were recently active on Meta platforms. Each day, we collected ads that were active or had recently been active, contained English ad text, and were delivered to users in regions covered by Meta’s Ad Transparency Library, primarily the EU and UK. While the ads themselves often promoted content intended for a global audience, our analysis focused on ads visible through the transparency framework rather than attempting to infer targeting outside of what the API explicitly exposes. We intentionally avoided narrowing the scope further in order to capture how scam advertising behaves at scale, not just in obvious edge cases.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis work exists because regulation forced visibility. In the EU and UK, ad transparency requirements expose advertiser behavior in ways that cannot be replicated elsewhere, enabling independent measurement of scam advertising at an industrial scale. Where such regulation is absent, scam activity does not disappear; it simply becomes harder to measure. Transparency does not create abuse; it reveals it.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor this analysis, we focused on consumer-facing ads and did not intentionally collect or analyze political advertising. Political ads are self-declared by advertisers, so while some may still appear in the dataset due to misclassification, they were not the target of this study.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eEven with these constraints, the dataset quickly became massive.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e14.5 million ads, billions of impressions, one pattern\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eOver a 23-day period, we collected \u003cstrong\u003e14.57 million ads\u003c/strong\u003e, representing \u003cstrong\u003e10.76 billion impressions\u003c/strong\u003e delivered across the EU and UK. Even as a partial view of the ecosystem, the scale was impossible to ignore.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eUsing intelligence and classification technology, we identified ads pointing to infrastructure associated with e-commerce scams, phishing campaigns, malware distribution and other consumer-facing threats. This allowed us to move beyond ad text and examine what users were actually being sent to.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe results were not subtle. \u003cstrong\u003e4.51 million ads\u003c/strong\u003e in our dataset were identified as scam-related, meaning \u003cstrong\u003enearly one in three ads (30.99%)\u003c/strong\u003e pointed to scam infrastructure. In total, these scam ads generated \u003cstrong\u003e143.8 million impressions in the EU\u003c/strong\u003e and \u003cstrong\u003e304.11 million impressions across the EU and UK\u003c/strong\u003e in less than a month.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is not an edge case. It is an\u003cstrong\u003e \u003c/strong\u003eincredible volume.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2026-02/Metascam1.jpg.webp?itok=iAC2JIBG 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":1076,"height":807},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2026-02/Metascam1.jpg.webp?itok=YN22y3R8 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":720,"height":540}],"caption":"","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2026-02/Metascam1.jpg.webp?itok=YN22y3R8","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003ch3\u003eWhy takedowns don’t scale\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eVolume alone does not explain why this problem persists. So, we looked at behavior over time.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAcross the dataset, we repeatedly observed scammers reusing the same infrastructure, identical domains, and near-identical ad text across many campaigns. In multiple cases, when one ad disappeared from the ad library for policy violations, other ads using the \u003cstrong\u003esame domain and messaging\u003c/strong\u003e remained active until their campaigns naturally expired.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFrom the outside, it seems enforcement of scam ads is reactive. Ads appear to be removed one at a time, often following reports or reviews, while related campaigns using the same infrastructure continue running. Even when scam components are reused at scale, generalization appears slow, even if they are delivered by the same advertiser.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe cannot see Meta’s internal detection signals, so we are careful not to assign intent. But the observable outcome is clear: \u003cstrong\u003eknown scam building blocks often remain usable long after one instance is taken down.\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAt this scale, that distinction matters.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2026-02/Metascamads2.jpg.webp?itok=KEUgAnaC 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":1076,"height":807},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2026-02/Metascamads2.jpg.webp?itok=bXWYWgGB 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":720,"height":540}],"caption":"","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2026-02/Metascamads2.jpg.webp?itok=bXWYWgGB","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003ch3\u003eA small number of advertisers cause outsized harm\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eScale tells us how big the problem is. Enforcement shows us why it persists.\u003cbr\u003eBut, to understand who is actually driving the harm, we asked a different question: are scam ads driven by countless small actors or by a smaller number of highly active criminal groups?\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe answer was clear.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eScam advertising was also highly concentrated. The \u003cstrong\u003etop 10 scam advertisers alone accounted for 56.1% of all scam ads\u003c/strong\u003e, representing \u003cstrong\u003e2.53 million unique scam advertisements\u003c/strong\u003e and \u003cstrong\u003e57.92 million impressions\u003c/strong\u003e. A relatively small number of advertiser entities were responsible for a majority of observed harm.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThese advertisers are not anonymous hobbyists. They are organized, persistent operators running industrial-scale campaigns.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe repeatedly traced clusters of campaigns back to payers and infrastructure linked to China and Hong Kong, operating fleets of short-lived pages created almost exclusively to run ads. Western brand names and English storefronts cycled rapidly, while payers and beneficiaries rotated or disappeared from disclosures altogether. What did not rotate were the mechanics: the same domains, URL patterns and ad behaviors resurfaced across supposedly unrelated advertisers.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eWhat comes next\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe numbers alone are unsettling. Millions of scam ads. Hundreds of millions of impressions. A small number of actors are driving disproportionate harm, repeatedly and at a scale.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis isn’t about imperfect moderation. It’s about a system that, in practice, allows attackers to move faster than constraints can keep up. In our next blog post, we’ll move this research from measurement to mechanics. We break down the techniques scammers use to hide malicious ads in plain sight, including how they manipulate displayed URLs, chain redirects, blend malicious links with legitimate ones and exploit the limits of transparency tools themselves.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBecause understanding the size of the problem is only the first step. Understanding how it persists is what allows it to be this.\u0026nbsp;\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003eYou are in a regular work call on Teams, Zoom or Google Meet. Someone clicks a button at the start, a little icon shows up saying “Recording and transcription on”, and everyone ignores it. You go through the agenda, say goodbye and most people leave.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThen someone says the classic, “Can a couple of you stay a minute to talk about next steps?”\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThat is usually when the real talk happens:\u0026nbsp;Who is really behind on a project, which customer might leave, where you will have to cut budget, who is getting promoted and who is not.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eNow the twist: in many tools, the AI transcript keeps running until the very last person leaves. Which means all that “after the meeting” talk is written down, and often shared with everyone who can see the meeting recap, including people who were not there at that moment.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eConvenient? Sure. Risky? Very.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is not just a big enterprise problem. For small and mid-sized businesses, one careless transcript can expose more than any single email.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eWhat auto transcripts actually do\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eModern video tools love giving you a “smart recap” of your calls:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eA full transcript of everything that was said \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eAutomatic notes, action items and highlights \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe option to read a meeting you never attended\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eFrom a user's point of view, this feels like magic. If you join late, you can scroll up and see what you missed. If you skip a call, you can read the summary instead of watching a one-hour recording.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBut there are a few details most people forget:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe transcript usually covers the whole call, not just the time you were in the room \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eIt often ends up stored in a shared space that more people can access than you think \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eIt is searchable text, very easy to copy, paste, forward and leak\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn short, that little “AI notes on” label means there is a silent participant that never forgets anything.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eWhy this is a big deal for small businesses\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf you run a small business, your meetings are often where the real strategy happens:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003ePricing decisions \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eSalary conversations \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eCustomer problems \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eSupplier issues \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eCash flow stress\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn a big corporation, there are policies, legal teams and IT departments that, hopefully, think about what gets recorded and for how long. In a small company, you might just click “try our new AI notes feature” and move on.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThat is where the danger starts. A few examples:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe finance call, where you talk openly about which roles could be cut if revenue drops, then the transcript is auto-shared with the whole team. \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe customer call where you discuss your real discount limits after the client leaves, and that text ends up in the same thread you share back with them. \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe incident response call, where you list all the weak points in your network, and that transcript lives in a shared drive that is poorly protected.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eNone of this requires a hacker genius. It just needs one person to forward the wrong link, a shared space to be misconfigured, or an account to be compromised.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eThe invisible audience problem\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eMost people look only at the faces in the meeting grid and think, “This is my audience”.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWith auto transcription, the real audience is bigger:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eEveryone who can see the meeting recap or transcript later \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eAny AI assistant that uses those transcripts to generate notes \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eAnyone who gets access to your collaboration tool if their account is compromised\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eOn top of that, transcripts do not show tone of voice, irony or frustration. A tired joke like “if this deal does not land we will have to fire half the team, haha” can look very different in a transcript taken out of context.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf you would not be happy seeing a sentence printed on a slide in front of the whole company, it should probably not live forever in a meeting transcript either.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eSo, what can you do, practically?\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eHere is a simple way to think about it that works both for employees and for small business owners.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e1. As an employee: treat the icon as a big red “Recorder on” light\u003c/strong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf the call says recording or transcription is on, assume:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eEverything you say can be read later by people who are not on the call now \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eYour words become searchable text that someone can forward or screenshot\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eTwo simple habits:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eBefore a sensitive side conversation, check if transcription is still on. If it is, ask the host to stop it or start a new call. \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eDo not say in a transcribed call what you would never write in a work email.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eIt is not about being paranoid; it is about not being surprised later.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e2. As a small business owner or manager\u003c/strong\u003e,\u003cstrong\u003e set a few clear rules\u003c/strong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eYou do not need a 20-page policy. Start with something very basic:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMeetings you never transcribe\u003c/strong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003cbr\u003eFor example, performance reviews, HR issues, layoffs, legal topics and security incidents. \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMeetings where transcription is OK, but with care\u003c/strong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003cbr\u003eTeam catch-ups, planning calls, customer calls. Here, agree that: \u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe host announces at the start that transcription is on \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe host stops it before any “only for a few of us” discussion starts \u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMeetings where transcription is actually helpful and low risk\u003c/strong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003cbr\u003eTraining, webinars and large briefings where the goal is to share information widely.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eWrite this in a short internal note or wiki page. People cannot follow rules they have never seen.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e3. Control where transcripts live and who can see them\u003c/strong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAsk your IT person or your most technical colleague to check:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eWhere are meeting transcripts stored by default? \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eWho can access them by default? Only participants, the whole team, “anyone with the link”? \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eAre there settings to limit how long they are kept?\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eEven just reducing “everyone forever” to “only participants for 90 days” makes a big difference.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e4. Be careful with “AI notetaker” bots\u003c/strong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSome tools let you invite a bot that joins your call to take notes. It looks cute, but it is also another account that can access your meetings and data.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBefore you allow them:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eCheck which company runs that bot \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eCheck their privacy policy and whether they use your data to train their models \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eDecide which types of meetings the bot is allowed to attend\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf you would not invite a stranger to sit in on a sensitive meeting, do not invite a bot either.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eMake a conscious choice, not an accidental one\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eAI transcripts are not evil. They help people who are hard of hearing, they help non native speakers, they make note-taking easier and they help people catch up on meetings they could not attend.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe problem is not the technology; it is autopilot.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf you are going to let an AI assistant listen to everything your company says on calls, do it with your eyes open:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eDecide which meetings it can listen to \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eDecide when it needs to stop listening \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eDecide who is allowed to read what is written\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eOtherwise, you might wake up one day and realize that the most honest, unfiltered moments in your business, the “stay a minute after the call” moments, have been quietly written down and shared much more widely than you ever points\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eAuraStealer is an emerging malware-as-a-service (MaaS) infostealer observed in recent Scam-Yourself campaigns.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eTo hinder both static and dynamic analysis, AuraStealer employs a wide range of anti-analysis and obfuscation techniques, including indirect control flow obfuscation and exception-driven API hashing.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eIn this technical blog post, we provide a deep dive into the malware’s execution flow and functionality and provide practical tips and workflows for defeating its obfuscation.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eTo support the defense community, Gen Threat Labs provides multiple scripts to assist defenders in analyzing AuraStealer, including a string decryption script and a dedicated configuration extractor.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003ch2\u003eIntroduction\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eAuraStealer is a rapidly growing infostealer-as-a-service, actively promoted across multiple underground forums since July 2025. The stealer is developed in C++ with a build size of ~500-700 kB and targets Windows systems from Windows 7 to Windows 11. It is marketed as a supposedly highly efficient, low-footprint stealer capable of stealing data from more than 110 browsers, 70 applications (including wallets and 2FA tools), as well as over 250 browser extensions, with the ability to further expand its collection scope through a customizable configuration. Contrary to the advertised claims, AuraStealer still contains multiple flaws that undermine its stealth and evasion capabilities, offering clear detection opportunities for defenders.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_01%20copy.jpg.webp?itok=eu_bI5eY 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":1076,"height":592},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_01%20copy.jpg.webp?itok=z5Zd256e 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":720,"height":396}],"caption":"Figure 1: AuraStealer promoted on an underground forum.","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_01%20copy.jpg.webp?itok=z5Zd256e","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eThe infostealer is offered through a tiered subscription model, with the \u003cstrong\u003eBasic\u003c/strong\u003e \u003cstrong\u003eplan\u003c/strong\u003e priced at $295/month and the \u003cstrong\u003eAdvanced\u003c/strong\u003e \u003cstrong\u003eplan\u003c/strong\u003e at $585/month. The tiers differ primarily in terms of configuration flexibility, data filtering, and operational scalability. A third tier, \u003cstrong\u003eTeam plan\u003c/strong\u003e, is currently under development and is expected to introduce features tailored for collaborative use. Additionally, a temporary \u003cstrong\u003eTrial tier\u003c/strong\u003e was also offered, priced at $165 for a two-week subscription, which included all the features of the \u003cstrong\u003eBasic plan\u003c/strong\u003e. The \u003cstrong\u003eTrial tier\u003c/strong\u003e was available for purchase only during a 30-day window, aiming to attract new users before they committed to a full subscription.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe subscriptions include access to a dedicated web panel for managing and viewing stolen data, built on the Tabler template. Given that both the panel and the stealer were originally offered exclusively in Russian, it is reasonable to assume the developers operate within Russian‑speaking cybercriminal communities. However, this does not necessarily indicate their precise origin. The panel has since been updated and now supports both Russian and English.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_02.jpeg.webp?itok=zH6rBsfy
