primarily spreads through Scam-Yourself campaigns, with\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://undercodenews.com/tiktoks-dark-trick-aura-stealer-malware-masquerades-as-product-activation-guides/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eTikTok videos\u003c/a\u003e disguised as product activation guides being one of them. In these videos, victims are lured by an apparently easy tutorial promising free activation of otherwise paid software. Viewers are instructed to manually retype and run a displayed command in an administrative PowerShell, which, however, instead of activating the software, quietly downloads and executes the malicious payload. Concrete examples of these videos, along with the full execution chain and additional IoCs, can be found\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://isc.sans.edu/diary/32380\" target=\"_blank\"\u003ehere\u003c/a\u003e.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eApart from TikTok Scam-Yourself campaigns, AuraStealer is also distributed through supposedly cracked games or software, with delivery chains of varying complexity. In the simplest cases, only a UPX-packed version of AuraStealer is executed, without additional stages, whereas the more elaborate campaigns employ multi-stage execution flows, involving custom loaders, DLL sideloading, injection into legitimate processes, or other intermediary steps. We have also observed AuraStealer being delivered together with GlassWorm via a malicious VS Code extension. Taken together, these observations indicate that AuraStealer is sold purely as the stealer itself, without a loader layer or additional delivery mechanisms.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eDetermining the exact scope of the AuraStealer threat is challenging, as we often block its delivery chains at earlier stages before the final payload can be executed. While the proactive defense is crucial for keeping our users safe, it also results in many AuraStealer payloads never reaching the system. As a result, it becomes inherently difficult not only to observe and quantify the full scope of the threat, but also to determine with certainty whether the blocked payloads were indeed intended to deliver AuraStealer.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eShould it, for example, be delivered through ClickFix, one of the most widely adopted delivery techniques lately, we would proactively defend against those attacks right at the clipboard stage with our\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/news/family-of-brands/clipboard-protection\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eClipboard protection\u003c/a\u003e feature. ClickFix typically refers to a technique in which users are presented with artificially generated errors or problems that must be resolved to continue, while simultaneously being provided with step-by-step instructions to address the issue. The steps usually include copying a command and running it via the Windows Run dialog or another system prompt, which, however, rather than resolving the issue, silently retrieves and executes the malicious payload.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo summarize, AuraStealer is not yet as widespread and popular as the more established infostealer families currently dominating the threat landscape, such as Lumma Stealer, StealC or Vidar. However, according to an\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://g0njxa.medium.com/approaching-stealers-devs-a-brief-interview-with-aura-9b513369e117\" target=\"_blank\"\u003einterview\u003c/a\u003e conducted by\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://x.com/g0njxa\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eg0njxa\u003c/a\u003e with the AuraStealer developers, the malware is backed by a committed team that continuously improves and evolves its functionality, aiming to become the number one in the infostealer market. This suggests that AuraStealer could become a more significant player in the future.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eAuraStealer Obfuscation\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eAuraStealer incorporates a wide range of techniques designed to hinder both static and dynamic analysis. These include control flow obfuscation, string encryption, constant obfuscation, as well as standard anti-debug, anti-tamper, and anti-VM checks. It further employs advanced techniques such as exception-driven API-hashing, leveraging the Heaven’s Gate for suspicious NTDLL calls, and performing checks to detect breakpoints on return addresses.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eIndirect Control Flow Obfuscation\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe first obfuscation technique an analyst is likely to encounter when opening AuraStealer in a disassembler, such as IDA Pro, is its indirect control flow obfuscation. The obfuscation is not only immediately noticeable, but also one of the most troublesome obstacles that must be overcome to properly analyze the stealer. The obfuscation is based on systematically replacing direct jumps and calls with indirect ones, where the actual target address is computed only at runtime. This method effectively disrupts static analysis, as the disassemblers are left with a set of seemingly unrelated basic blocks, effectively breaking any control-flow graph analysis or decompilation.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo demonstrate how this obfuscation works in practice, and how it can be defeated, we focus on the\u0026nbsp;\u003ccode\u003eWinMain\u003c/code\u003e function, which is both the longest and the most complex function of the stealer. Several examples illustrating the obfuscation mechanism at the assembly level are shown in Figure 4 below, with the instructions relevant to computing each target address highlighted in a distinct color. Notably, the screenshot also illustrates that the obfuscation successfully confused IDA, causing it to misinterpret the entire\u0026nbsp;\u003ccode\u003eWinMain\u003c/code\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/em\u003efunction as a single basic block.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_04.jpeg.webp?itok=htfTd3y- 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":722,"height":884},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_04.jpeg.webp?itok=0zXZLzj_ 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":720,"height":882}],"caption":"Figure 4: Disassembled code of the WinMain function, obfuscated with indirect control flow obfuscation. First, the most basic scheme used to obfuscate function calls, which relies solely on the sum of two values can be resolved with a simple trick in IDA Pro. In these cases, it is sufficient to hint to IDA that the offsets contributing to the sum operation are in fact constant. this trick alone is insufficient to address the conditional jumps and other schemes of AuraStealer's indirect control flow obfuscation. Nonetheless, given how many calls are obfuscated with this simple scheme, applying it still provides meaningful progress and can serve as a solid starting point for further deobfuscation. Moreover, AuraStealer applies the same obfuscation scheme to hide certain function arguments as well, making it applicable for those cases as well.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo tackle the more sophisticated patterns, \u003cstrong\u003ebackward slicing\u003c/strong\u003e proved to be the most effective approach. For those interested in a more detailed explanation of how this technique works, the articles \u003ca href=\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/lummac2-obfuscation-through-indirect-control-flow\" target=\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cem\u003eLummaC2: Obfuscation Through Indirect Control Flow\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/a\u003e by Nino Isakovic and Chuong Dong, and \u003ca href=\"https://cyber.wtf/2025/11/19/rhadamanthys-loader-deobfuscation/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cem\u003eRhadamanthys Loader Deobfuscation\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/a\u003e by Melissa Eckardt provide excellent explanations and are definitely worth reading.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn short, backward slicing is a program analysis technique that identifies instructions that may influence a specific register or memory address at a specific program point by following data and control dependencies. The resulting set of instructions (also referred to as “slice”) can then be used for further analysis, such as emulation or symbolic execution, to recover the value of the targeted register or memory location.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn our case, we employed a heuristic heavily inspired by backward slicing but simplified and tailored specifically for AuraStealer. The heuristic can be summarized in the following steps:\u003c/p\u003e\u003col\u003e\u003cli\u003eLocate the indirect \u003ccode\u003ejmp/call\u003c/code\u003e instruction. \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eAdd the register used as the target operand of this indirect \u003ccode\u003ejmp/call\u003c/code\u003e instruction to the \u003ccode\u003etracked_regs\u003c/code\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/em\u003eset.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eIterate through instructions in reverse (from the indirect \u003ccode\u003ejmp/call\u003c/code\u003e towards lower addresses), analyzing each instruction whether it affects any of the tracked registers.\u003col\u003e\u003cli\u003eIf an instruction \u003cstrong\u003emodifies\u003c/strong\u003e a tracked register, mark it as \u003cstrong\u003erelevant\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eFor a relevant instruction, determine if it completely overwrites a tracked register (e.g., \u003ccode\u003eMOV reg, imm\u003c/code\u003e, \u003ccode\u003eXOR reg, reg\u003c/code\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e,\u0026nbsp;\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/em\u003eetc.), and if so, remove that register from \u003ccode\u003etracked_reg\u003c/code\u003e, ending its dependency chain.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eOtherwise, identify all source registers of that relevant instruction and add them to the \u003ccode\u003etracked_reg\u003c/code\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/em\u003eset to propagate the dependency further back.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ol\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eStop the process if any of these conditions occur:\u003col\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe \u003ccode\u003etracked_regs\u003c/code\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/strong\u003eset is empty (all dependencies have been resolved).\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eA control-flow instruction is encountered (\u003ccode\u003ecall\u003c/code\u003e, \u003ccode\u003ejmp\u003c/code\u003e, \u003ccode\u003eret\u003c/code\u003e), indicating a basic block boundary.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eA maximum instruction limit is reached (fail-safe).\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ol\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eFinally, return the address of the earliest (lowest address) relevant instruction found, marking the start of the slice.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ol\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor clarity, this heuristic is not meant to implement complete backward slicing. It deliberately omits memory‑write tracking and focuses solely on register flows. However, it is important to note that this limitation is intentional, as register-level analysis is sufficient for the deobfuscation of the indirect control flow obfuscation employed by AuraStealer. We use the heuristic solely to identify the slice’s starting address, from which we then perform symbolic execution with Angr to compute the target address(es). That said, if AuraStealer’s obfuscation were to change, the heuristic could be readily extended to also account for memory interactions.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe reason we chose symbolic execution over emulation is that symbolic execution is inherently designed to explore all possible execution paths, making it easier to implement. In terms of computational requirements, it remains feasible since we only symbolically execute small code chunks.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAttentive readers may have noticed that this approach has a minor imperfection. The heuristic terminates upon encountering a \u003ccode\u003ecall\u003c/code\u003e instruction. However, as shown in Figure 5, the target address of some \u003ccode\u003ejmps\u003c/code\u003e can depend on the results of previous function calls, which may lead to an incorrectly identified slice start. To handle such cases, whenever a register is unconstrained, we assign it a symbolic boolean value (0 or 1), under the assumption that any function influencing such jumps returns a boolean value. Such cases, however, are relatively rare.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe whole process can be automated with a bit of scripting and the recovered target addresses can be pushed back into IDA as comments, which already gives a good baseline for navigating the code.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_11_0.jpeg.webp?itok=xVeq5AHc 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":1076,"height":867},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_11_0.jpeg.webp?itok=8dhhCtuk 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":720,"height":580}],"caption":"  Figure 11: Recovered target addresses set as comments in IDA Pro.","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_11_0.jpeg.webp?itok=8dhhCtuk","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eHowever, we can go further – our real goal is to make IDA reconstruct the original control flow graph, not just annotate it. Once the target addresses of the indirect \u003ccode\u003ejmps/calls\u003c/code\u003e are retrieved, there are several ways to proceed. One option would be to patch the binary. However, due to the variability of the patterns involved and the presence of an anti‑tamper protection in AuraStealer, we have opted for a different approach.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eInstead of patching the binary, which is destructive and easily detectable by malware, we leveraged the IDA SDK to hook directly into the instruction analysis process. For this purpose, IDA provides \u003ca href=\"https://cpp.docs.hex-rays.com/structpost__event__visitor__t.html\" target=\"_blank\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003epost_event_visitor_t\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/a\u003e, a class that allows listening and reacting to specific events in IDA by overriding the virtual function \u003ccode\u003ehandle_post_event\u003c/code\u003e.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_12.jpeg.webp?itok=86TkldZY 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":1076,"height":107},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_12.jpeg.webp?itok=TnbqbesL 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":720,"height":72}],"caption":"Figure 12: The declaration of the handle_post_event virtual function.","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_12.jpeg.webp?itok=TnbqbesL","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eAmong all the possible event codes, the interesting one is the \u003ccode\u003eprocessor_t::ev_ana_insn\u003c/code\u003e\u0026nbsp;(analyze instruction), which is triggered right after IDA calls the processor module’s instruction decoder, but before the resulting \u003ccode\u003einsn_t\u003c/code\u003e structure is finalized and committed to the database. At that point, we can inspect, or even modify, the decoded instruction. In other words, we can alter IDA’s perception of the code without ever touching the underlying binary.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAt this point, several observations are worth highlighting:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe indirect calls almost always have a single target.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eMany conditional jumps have only two targets, one of which is the immediately following instruction.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eResolving the indirect call obfuscation is therefore straightforward. Whenever we intercept the \u003ccode\u003eprocessor_t::ev_ana_insn\u003c/code\u003e event, we check whether the analyzed instruction is an indirect call (\u003ccode\u003ecall reg\u003c/code\u003e). If the target address is known and it is the only possible target, we replace the \u003ccode\u003ecall reg\u003c/code\u003e instruction with \u003ccode\u003ecall \u0026lt;calculated_target_address\u0026gt;\u003c/code\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor indirect conditional jumps, it would, in principle, be possible to determine the exact branch type by analyzing the conditional move used to compute the target address and replacing the \u003ccode\u003ejmp\u003c/code\u003e instruction with the corresponding conditional jump instruction. However, we opted for a much simpler and more pragmatic strategy. Many of these indirect jumps are, in fact, only obfuscated loops or \u003ccode\u003eif-then/if-then-else\u003c/code\u003e constructs with one of the target address pointing to the immediately following instruction. So, whenever we encounter an indirect \u003ccode\u003ejmp\u003c/code\u003e instruction with precisely two possible targets, one of which is the fall-through address, we replace the \u003ccode\u003ejmp\u003c/code\u003e instruction with \u003ccode\u003ejnz\u003c/code\u003e (jump if not zero) pointing to the non-fall-through target address.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis transformation does not yield a perfectly accurate decompilation. However, if we accept the fact that some comparisons may appear as\u0026nbsp;\u003ccode\u003econdition != 0\u003c/code\u003e rather than their exact form, we end up with a control flow that is already much easier to follow. Moreover, whenever the precise condition semantics matter, the original condition can always be examined directly in the disassembly. Although it would be possible to reconstruct and substitute the exact conditional jump for every case, we do not believe it is worth the effort.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_13.jpeg.webp?itok=DA2cm_PF that due to the heavy control\u0026nbsp;flow obfuscation with many references to code locations, IDA often struggles to recognize proper function boundaries. It is thus often necessary to manually adjust where the function begins and ends to ensure IDA analyses the whole function.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eException-Driven API Hashing\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo obfuscate its usage of WinAPI functions, AuraStealer employs the API hashing technique. For these purposes, it first resolves all the functions required by the binary via PEB-walking and builds two hash-based lookup tables that maps hashes to XOR-masked addresses.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe reason for maintaining two tables is that AuraStealer does not invoke these function directly. Instead, it deliberately triggers an exception, which, however, is intercepted by a custom exception handler. The handler inspects the address from which the exception originates and uses that information to dispatch the appropriate function address.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo resolve a specific function, AuraStealer takes the precomputed \u003ccode\u003eMurmurHash3\u003c/code\u003e value (seeded with \u003ccode\u003e0xDEADBEEF\u003c/code\u003e) of the function’s name, re-hashes it with \u003ccode\u003eFNV-1a\u003c/code\u003e, and uses both values to query the first precomputed lookup table. The query returns the XOR-masked address from which the exception is to be thrown, together with the mask used to protect it. AuraStealer then unmasks the address and makes a call to it, intentionally throwing an \u003ccode\u003eEXCEPTION_ACCESS_VIOLATION\u003c/code\u003e, which is intercepted by the custom exception handler.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe exception handler rehashes the originating address using \u003ccode\u003eFNV-1a\u003c/code\u003e and uses both values to query the second precomputed lookup table. This query returns the XOR-masked address of the actual target function, again along with the mask. The two values are XORed together and the resulting address is loaded into the \u003ccode\u003eEIP\u003c/code\u003e register, effectively invoking the requested WinAPI function.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_15.jpeg.webp?itok=kowkaGle 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":1076,"height":1008},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_15.jpeg.webp?itok=Gw3InX6R 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":720,"height":675}],"caption":"Figure 15: Visualized exception-driven API hashing.","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_15.jpeg.webp?itok=Gw3InX6R","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eThe MurmurHash3 values of the target functions are further obfuscated using an XOR mask (see Figure 16). In this example, \u003ccode\u003e0x130B0F3C ^ 0x25A36E25 = 0x36A86119\u003c/code\u003e, which is equal to the value returned by \u003ccode\u003eMurmurHash3(“GetModuleHandleA”, seed: 0xDEADBEEF\u003c/code\u003e)\u003cem\u003e.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/em\u003eSince the result value is produced only by a XOR of two constants, IDA’s decompiler can optimize the expression and directly display the deobfuscated value (see Figure 17).\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis allows us to precompute the hashes for all API-hashed functions and replace the decompiler-computed constants with the corresponding function names. However, it is important to note that this approach relies on IDA’s decompiler, which may produce incomplete results if the control flow is not fully deobfuscated. That said, the constants could also be recovered by combining backward slicing to isolate the instructions that influence the final constant value, and emulating them with an emulation framework (for example, Unicorn). In this case, removing the obfuscation would be considerably easier than for the indirect control flow obfuscation, as the constant obfuscation is merely an XOR, requiring emulation of only a few instructions. Therefore, the necessary context is distributed across the entire function. Depending on that outcome, it either performs or skips additional checks, which include an anti-tampering check and a dialog box check.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eAnti-Tampering Check\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe anti-tampering check is done using the \u003ccode\u003eMapFileAndCheckSumW\u003c/code\u003e WinAPI function, which computes the file’s checksum and compares it against the value stored in the PE header. If those values differ, AuraStealer stops its execution.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe purpose of this check is to prevent analysts from patching or otherwise altering the executable. With that in mind, analysts should be cautious about any changes made to the binary (including software breakpoints) and remain vigilant during dynamic analysis, as the malware may deliberately exhibit misleading behavior if tampering is detected.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eDialog Box Check\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eSimilar to what \u003ca href=\"https://outpost24.com/blog/lummac2-anti-sandbox-technique-trigonometry-human-detection/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eLumma\u003c/a\u003e, and more recently also \u003ca href=\"https://research.checkpoint.com/2025/rhadamanthys-0-9-x-walk-through-the-updates/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eRhadamanthys\u003c/a\u003e, does, if AuraStealer detects that it is being run unprotected, it displays a dialog box prompting the user to enter a code to continue. Unlike Lumma and Rhadamanthys, it requires the user not only to click the OK button but also to correctly enter the displayed code. On the other hand, it does not display any warning message that malware is to be run, which is what both Lumma and Rhadamanthys display in the message box.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_27.jpeg.webp?itok=S_XSIdFH 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":262,"height":149},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_27.jpeg.webp?itok=LE2ksud8 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":262,"height":149}],"caption":"Figure 27: The AuraStealer’s dialog box displayed when running unprotected.","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_27.jpeg.webp?itok=LE2ksud8","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eThis mechanism halts AuraStealer’s execution until the correct value is provided and is aimed at forcing the malware distributors to deliver the stealer with an additional protective layer. However, we were unable to confirm that claim.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eAnti-Sandbox Checks\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf all previous checks pass, the process continues to the anti-sandbox stage, which comprises three primary checks.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFirst, AuraStealer evaluates whether the \u003ccode\u003eSleep\u003c/code\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/em\u003efunction has been hooked or modified. The script is publicly accessible on our GitHub.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eHuman Checks\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe human check comprises two subchecks, both implemented as infinite loops.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe first check monitors whether the user input changes over time. It begins by measuring the number of ticks between two \u003ccode\u003eGetTickCount\u003c/code\u003e calls with a \u003ccode\u003eSleep(1000 ms)\u003c/code\u003e in between. A while loop then repeatedly performs a \u003ccode\u003eSleep(500 ms)\u003c/code\u003e followed by a call to \u003ccode\u003eGetLastInputInfo\u003c/code\u003e, which returns a \u003ccode\u003eLASTINPUTINFO\u003c/code\u003e structure. This structure contains a variable, \u003ccode\u003edwTime\u003c/code\u003e, representing the tick count at which the last input event occurred. Then, another call to \u003ccode\u003eGetTickCount\u003c/code\u003e is made, and \u003ccode\u003edwTime\u003c/code\u003e is subtracted from it. If the result is less than the previously measured number of ticks measured by the one-second \u003ccode\u003eSleep\u003c/code\u003e, the check passes. Otherwise, the loop repeats until the check passes.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe second check begins by a call to \u003ccode\u003eGetForegroundWindow\u003c/code\u003e, which returns a handle to the current foreground window. A while loop then repeatedly calls \u003ccode\u003eSleep(500 ms)\u003c/code\u003e and checks \u003ccode\u003eGetForegroundWindow\u003c/code\u003e again. If the returned handle differs from the initial one, the check passes. Otherwise, the loop continues until the foreground window changes.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eAnti-VM Checks\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe Anti‑VM check consists of multiple subchecks. First, the presence of a hypervisor is checked using the \u003ccode\u003ecpuid\u003c/code\u003e instruction. When \u003ccode\u003ecpuid\u003c/code\u003e is executed with \u003ccode\u003eEAX = 1\u003c/code\u003e, the 31st bit of \u003ccode\u003eECX\u003c/code\u003e indicates the environment – it is \u003ccode\u003e0\u003c/code\u003e on a physical machine and \u003ccode\u003e1\u003c/code\u003e on a virtual machine.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eNext, a combination of the WinAPI functions \u003ccode\u003eGetDesktopWindow\u003c/code\u003e and \u003ccode\u003eGetWindowRect\u003c/code\u003e is used to obtain the \u003ccode\u003eright\u003c/code\u003e variable from the resulting \u003ccode\u003eRECT\u003c/code\u003e structure, which is then compared against \u003ccode\u003e1024\u003c/code\u003e (it is expected to be greater). Finally, \u003ccode\u003eGlobalMemoryStatusEx\u003c/code\u003e is called, and the \u003ccode\u003eullTotalPhys\u003c/code\u003e variable of the returned \u003ccode\u003eMEMORYSTATUSEX\u003c/code\u003e structure is checked to ensure it is not \u003ccode\u003e0\u003c/code\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf all of these checks pass, AuraStealer calls \u003ccode\u003eGetSystemInfo\u003c/code\u003e and inspects the returned \u003ccode\u003eSYSTEM_INFO\u003c/code\u003e structure to verify that \u003ccode\u003edwNumberOfProcessors\u003c/code\u003e is not less than four. Generated Mutex\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo prevent multiple instances from running simultaneously, AuraStealer utilizes dynamically generated mutexes based on build-specific and time-dependent values. The malware first loads the \u003ccode\u003ebuild_id\u003c/code\u003e value from its configuration (for example, \u003ccode\u003ea0858933-16a7-433f-a9cc-68490ace0576\u003c/code\u003e) and computes a \u003ccode\u003edjb2\u003c/code\u003e hash over it. The hash is then added to the result of an integer division \u003ccode\u003e_Xtime_get_ticks()/36000000000\u003c/code\u003e, which effectively produces a value that changes once per hour. The final sum serves as a seed for a Mersenne-Twister pseudorandom number generator, which is used to generate a random alphanumeric string between 16 and 32 characters in length. The generated string is prepended with \u003ccode\u003eGlobal\\\u003c/code\u003e, forming the final mutex name.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_45.jpeg.webp?itok=oFS-2g35 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":810,"height":389},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_45.jpeg.webp?itok=TI9zq9bE 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":720,"height":346}],"caption":"Figure 45: AuraStealer's mutex creation (decompiled code).","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_45.jpeg.webp?itok=TI9zq9bE","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eThe fact that the mutex changes every hour highlights that the stealer is designed to run only for a short period of time – a trend that has become increasingly common among modern infostealers. The goal is to execute briefly, steal as much data as possible, and, preferably, remove all traces of its presence.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eApplication-Bound Encryption Bypass\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo extract sensitive data from Chromium-based browsers, AuraStealer must first overcome Application-Bound Encryption (ABE), which it achieves by spawning the target browser in headless mode using \u003ccode\u003eCreateProcessW\u003c/code\u003e, with the process created in a suspended state, followed by a code injection. From within the injected context, the malware invokes \u003ccode\u003eIElevator::Decrypt\u003c/code\u003e to decrypt the \u003ccode\u003eapp_bound_encrypted_key\u003c/code\u003e, which enables the decryption of any ABE-protected data, including passwords and cookies.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_46.jpeg.webp?itok=NjlxNOxR 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":762,"height":151},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_46.jpeg.webp?itok=koba6vpa 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":720,"height":143}],"caption":"Figure 46: AuraStealer’s ABE bypass.","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_46.jpeg.webp?itok=koba6vpa","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eThe injection itself is implemented by creating a shared section via \u003ccode\u003eNtCreateSection\u003c/code\u003e and mapping it into the target browser process using \u003ccode\u003eNtMapViewOfSection\u003c/code\u003e, after which the injected payload is executed using \u003ccode\u003eNtCreateThreadEx\u003c/code\u003e. All of the NTDLL calls involved in the injection are executed through Heaven’s Gate, a technique that allows 64-bit code to be executed from a 32-bit process on a 64-bit OS. This ensures both compatibility with 64-bit browsers and also helps evade detection.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_47.jpeg.webp?itok=M2ft3vYC 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":483,"height":185},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_47.jpeg.webp?itok=qwFnafmQ 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":483,"height":185}],"caption":"Figure 47: AuraStealer’s usage of Heaven's Gate.","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_47.jpeg.webp?itok=qwFnafmQ","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eThe injected payload is a position-independent shellcode, slightly modified at runtime for each target browser process. These two files serve as an inter-process communication mechanism between AuraStealer and the injected shellcode. The first file is used to pass the encrypted \u003ccode\u003eapp_bound_encrypted_key\u003c/code\u003e to the injected shellcode, while the second file is used by the shellcode to write back the decrypted key for retrieval by the stealer.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAlthough AuraStealer is capable of decrypting the \u003ccode\u003eapp_bound_encrypted\u003c/code\u003e key, required to decrypt appbound-encrypted data, from certain Chromium-based browsers, it still does not work reliably across all of them, suggesting that the product has not yet reached a fully polished state.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eNetwork Traffic\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eAuraStealer’s network traffic can be divided into four stages. The stealer first verifies internet connectivity by checking the reachability of \u003ccode\u003e1.1.1.1:53\u003c/code\u003e (Cloudflare). Upon success, it attempts to establish communication with its C2 infrastructure by querying the \u003ccode\u003e/api/live\u003c/code\u003e endpoint and expecting a response\u003ccode\u003e true\u003c/code\u003e.
